The Football Association          —   —-

Dear Sir,
            RE: Inquiry into Hillsborough
     W Y is one KEY factor being ignored ?          One that got attention recently,in
other matches (London & Manchester).
     Delaying the turn-out of players, and the kick-off,VDULD have de-fused the
situation.
     Bxcited people OUTSIDE hear the cheers as the teams come onto the pitch.
     Those outside become TERY impatient, and agitated.     the potential for
mass hysteria exists. All they want to do is to get inside.
     Police THINK in terms of "riot shields" \tien tnings get out of hand....
or resort to "paniw measures"^ such as opening tnat g^te} exascercating problems.
     Baphoria, if not panic,sets-in ! All rush blindly, without a care to any
potential danger..... they LITE for the minute.... ..the ms-tch nas started AND
they might miss that vital   goal. Absence of steward/police "guidance* was awful.
     Nobody is considering   the psychological effects upon tnose OUTSIDE#let
alone that INSIEE is a "HEAD-END" (for m j r O l human behaviour was never considered.
     A delayed kick-off, with a mega-phone message for those OUTSIDE,would have
eased the situation there...... those at the REAR being eased to safety, tnus
taking the pressure off the front.
     WHO is responsible for co-ordinating the appearance of players (and thus
the kick-off), with the problems of getting spectators into the ground safely ?
     VJhat is the use of a central police control unit, if there is no overall
co-ordination ? Many Police (as seen on television) seaned banused and helpless.
                                 Yours sincerely,




PS/ I F D telephone "I.T.Y. "Hie Time...The Place" about S-JO am; and a person
from London UDD bring-up this matter of a delayed kick-off. Much emphasis is on
the lack of Police awareness. A Journalist H D foresee the brewing danger. Many
fams screamed for help, but there was NO feed-system of any Kind. Complacency
prevailed, even tiien people did escape "over the fence". Vtiat use was closed T.T. 1%

    their "stress-reiease" out-let. Take that away, and you will induce SOCIAL
problems elsewhere. It could induce MDrii general hooliganism & mis-behaviour.
Remember that grounds usea to cope with 70,000 plus, not a mere 55,000.

                             %
                                                Surrey




15 April 1989




Dear Sirs

I have just witnessed with growing horror the unfolding story of the
Hillsborough tragedy.

Until he died 10 years ago my father,)               |was a lifelong
devotee of 'Wednesday' and w a s |
            [ He would have been devastated by this tragedy, this
appalling but inevitable event which filled our television screens.

We simply cannot allow the competition to run its course this year. It
would mock those who were killed and further alienate the millions who are
increasingly contemptuous of football and its culture.

There would be no winners. Neither of the semi finalists would have real
heart for the game and to continue in the spirit of 'playing for those who
died' or 'the show must go on' would be the worst kind of hypocrisy.

I beg the Association to abandon the remainder of the F.A.Cup tournament
and let 1989 be remembered as the year of No Final as a monument to our
collective stupidity.

As a further reminder to all players, fans and administrators, start off
next season with a three way tournament between Forest, Liverpool and
Everton and give every penny taken at the gates to the fund which must be
set up to provide for those bereaved.

Yours faithfully

Dear    Mr    Kelly,

H a v i n g j u s t w a t c h e d on t h e   television        the    tragic      scenes         from
Hillsborough,             I     felt   compelled        to w r i t e to y o u c o n c e r n i n g the
s c e n e s I w i t n e s s e d as a s p e c t a t o r at t he 1961 F A C u p S e m i - f i n a l
between       Spurs     and W o l v e s   at   the    same    ground.

S p u r s s u p p o r t e r s ( i n c l u d i n g myself!) w e r e a l s o a l l o c a t e d t he
L e p p i n g s L a n e end, of t h e g r o u n d , m u c h to o u r d i s g u s t at the              time
c o n s i d e r i n g the s i z e of s u p p o r t of t h e t w o c l u b s i n v o l v e d .

The c r u s h of t h e c r o w d , a l t h o u g h not as         serious     as     todays,     was
s t i l l g r e a t e n o u g h to c a u s e c o n c e r n to the e x t e n t t h a t l a r g e
numbers           of  supporters         were      withdrawn         from    the S p u r s e n d a n d
p l a c e d a l o n g the c i n d e r s t r a c k a r o u n d the e d g e of t h e p i t c h f o r
the d u r a t i o n of the g a m e .          Some s u p p o r t e r s were even r e m o ve d      to
the       opposite        e n d of the g r o u n d a n d c o r d o n e d o f f f r o m the W o l v e s
s u p p o r t e r s by a l i n e of p o l i c e m e n .

This       I believe,           i n d i c a t e s that too m a n y t i c k e t s    a r e a l l o c a t e d to
the        Leppings         L a n e e n d of t he g r o u n d .    Today's          incidents surely
b e a r t h i s out.           It m u s t a l s o be c o m m o n s e n s e       to     allocate          more
t i c k e t s to the l a r g e r c l u b i n v o l v e d in a n y s e m i - f i n a l ,         something
w h i c h o b v i o u s l y d o e s not o c c u r at p r e s e n t .

It is s u r e l y      now,      t i m e to c o n s i d e r r e m o v i n g t h e     fencing          around
many grounds           which I         s u s p e c t , w a s o n e of th e  the     m a j o r  c a u s e s for
such a large           l o s s of      life.          What w o u l d have   h a p p e n e d   at   Brad'ford
if t h e r e h a d     b e e n f e n c i n g on t h a t s t a n d ?

 I w o u l d a l s o l i k e to      put to y o u t h e i d e a of a         second             national
 s t a d i u m in t h e N o r t h    of E n g l a n d to c a t e r not o n l y f o r s e m i - f i n a l s
 and       finals        involving northern              clubs      but  big enough also for
 m a t c h e s i n v o l v i n g the E n g l i s h n a t i o n a l team.

 I look      forward      to   any    reply     from     you .

 Yours       sincerely

Sir

Following the tragic events at Hillsborough, I wou ld like         to
express my own view on what may be a co nt rib utory factor.

In February 1985 I took a half day off from             my business
(Insurance Broker) to travel to Sund erland to see the away leg
of Chelsea's League Cup semi-final.      The train was both filthy
and devoid of heati ng (the tempe rature was well below freezing)
- obviously ex-rolling stock whic h I un de rs ta nd because British
Rail have had some per fe ct ly good trains w r ec ke d wh ilst on
football  duty.     The  train was   stopped    outside   Sunderland
Station for an hour.    This is also underst an dab le as the local
police may have decided that they could do wit hou t trainloads
of visiting supporters in the town prior to the match.         What I
don't understand, and find unacceptable, are the events which
followed.

The occupants of the train were she pherded by           a roundabout
route so as to arrive at the ground after the kic k-off only to
find that 7 out of 12 turnstiles were closed.              The mayhem
outside the ground was a min or version of the situat ion at
Hillsborough.     Whi lst   there   was    no  voilence,     the  fans
(including   myself)   were    angry    and   frustrat ed   at   their
treatment.

Once acess was gained into the stand, I discovered, at           full
time, that the only exit doors at the rear of the stand          were
locked.
Back at the station after the game, the mo unted police saw fit
to control the wa iti ng supporters by riding their horses into
them, causing unnecessary crushing and panic.

The parallel between these    unreported incidents and the tragedy
of Hillsborough is that      football supporters are treated as
second class citizens.         The major decisions taken in both
cases appear to have been     for the benefit of the police w it h
little, if any, regard for    the supporters.

Yours    thful ly




cc      K B a t e s Es q
        Chel sea Football   Cl u b
                                                        ( f r r ^

                                  London

                                   18 April 1989.


Dear Mr Kelly,
I an an Everton fan, and am obviously very dismayed by the
events at Hillsborough.
Although I believe that this season's FA Cup should become a
memorial to the fans I do not think it should be completed
in the normal way. It is unfair to expect the players of any
of the three remaining teams to compete properly for a
trophy that has lost its value in any footballing sense.

I feel a more fitting tribute to the victims would be to
stage a match between two teams made up of players from all
three clubs. Thus no one wins or loses and football will
have come together for the benefit of the bereaved.

Yours sincerely,
Football Association Ltd.
16 Lancaster Gate
W2



                                                         17.4.1989
Dear Sirs,

In the light of the dreadful tragedy that occured on Saturday at Hillsborough,
as a Millwal1 supporter who was in attendance at the infamous Luton game,
I would request that some attention is given to the footage immediately
prior to that which is normally shown.
The 'incident' was sparked off because the supporters were being crushed into
an area not nearly large enough for them. During the minutes before the 'riot'
occured,there were old men,young women and children climbing over spiked fences
in order to avoid being crushed, and to get on to the pitch for safety.
Admittedly this was still no excuse for the damage subsequently caused to the
ground,but had deaths occured,as did on Saturday,the story would have been a
very different one. Instead the Millwal1 v. Luton match has been used as an
instance of everything bad within football and synonomous with crowd violence,
without the cause ever having been disussed.
The Luton police and ground officials were saved having to answer the pressing
questions that regrettably the Hillsborough authorities will have to answer.
If more attention had been given to that aspect of the Millwall 'riot' as it is
so often referred to, maybe these recent deaths could have been avoided.
Instead too many people,authorities and media alike have been only too happy
to capitalise on the 'MI 1lwal1' phenomenon and been prepared to wait for deaths
before looking more closely at crowd management.

Yours sincerely,_ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _ _

D e a r Sir,
                             Football disaster.

T h i s l e t t e r w i l l u n d o u b t e d l y be i g n o r e d , b u t it is
n e c e s s a r y to c o m p l a i n a b o u t t h e t o t a l i n s e n s t i v i t y
of t h e F A in r e a r r a n g i n g c u p m a t c h e s w i t h so
m a n y s u p p p o r t e r s s t i l l in h o s p i t a l a nd no f u n e r a l s
h a v i n g t a k e n p l a c e . F o l l o w i n g t h e r e m a r k s of t h e
C h a i r m a n of L i v e r p o o l F C y o u c h a n g e d y o u r d e c i s i o n
b u t it s h o u l d h a v e n o t b e e n n e c e s s a r y .
A r s e n a l f o o t b a l c l u b , w i t h t h e a g r e e m e n t of W i m b l e d o n ,
c a n c e l l e d t h e i r m a t c h l a s t n i g h t ( M o n d a y ) a nd t h e r e
is t a l k of t h e c l u b b e i n g d e a l t w i t h by d i s c i p l i n a r y
m e t h o d s . If y o u so do t h i s t h e n I t h i n k it t o t a l l y
i m p r o p e r an d a g a i n w o u l d s h o w a l a c k of f e e l i n g w h e n
o t h e r s are showing the g r e a t e s t respect.
W i t h r e s p e c t , w h a t h a v e y o u a c t u a l l y d o n e to
m a k e f o o tball a day out for f a m i l i e s . You o p p o s e
G o v e r n m e n t p r o p o s a l s but to dat e I have not seen
a n y t h i n g c o n s t r u c t i v e f r o m y o u r s i d e - it is
p § % 6 i b l e th at I m i s s e d it b t so h a v e m a n y o t h e r
people apparently.
F o o t b a l l in t h i s c o u n t r y is in a d e p l o r a b l e s t a t e
w h e r e y o u a l l o w m i l l i o n s of p o u n d s to be s p e n t
on p l a y e r s b u t m o d e r n i s a t i o n of g r o u n d s b e i n g
i g n o r e d . On t e l e v i s i o n y o u s p o k e of s e a t i n g
o n l y , w h y h a v e y o u h a v e w a i t e d so l o n g ?
T h e r e is v e r y l i t t l e c o n f i d e n c e in t h e F A n o w -
is it n o t t i m e f o r c h a n g e s b r i n g i n g a m o d e r n
o u t l o o k . No o f f e n c e m e a n t b u t t h i n g s do h a v e to
alter.




The Chief E x e c u t i v e

                                                        Dallas, Texas
                                                        U.S.A.
                                                        (Until 31st May 1989)


                                                        Walsall
                                                        ( After 1st June)

                                                        April 17th 1989
Dear Sir,

I listened with great sadness to the B B C world service on 15th April as I
heard, live, of the fatalities at Hillsbrough, Sheffield. And later I saw with
horror, the pictures on television.
Having been in Texas for 12 months I have had an opportunity to attend
various sporting events and having seen over 6oo soccer games in Great
Britain, in the process attending over 40 grounds, I naturally made some
comparison. Texas sporting events have some common elements. If in
describing them I seem over-critical of British Stadiums forgive me. M y
comments arise out of a deep concern and a love for British Football.
Saturday's event left me sick and deeply upset.
At sporting events here one can park right outside the stadium. Of course
the Americans have the advantage of space, but it makes it very easy to
park so close.
Inside the stadium one notices major contrasts even with the very best of
football grounds in Great Britain. A major difference is that everyone has a
seat, and a comfortable seat at that, with a clear view of the match,
spectator facilities are vastly superior in the States. You are made to feel
welcome and wanted at events here. For all m y love of football, I am not
sure I have experienced that. The relationship of soccer club to supporters
is an uncomfortable one, I feel. In Dallas you feel as if you have a personal
welcome. Part of this is due to the way the sport is presented here. You are
told what is going on, encouraged to participate in a partisan but fair way.
I think that the sports I have witnessed, American Football,Indoor Soccerm
Baseball and Basketball are vastly inferior games to British Soccer; yet in
terms of presentation they have us beaten hands down.
If you want refreshments, refreshment points are plentiful; and there is
plenty of choice, and the quality of the foods is very good. Incidently,
alchohol is sold at sporting events in Texas.
Toilet facilities at British soccer grounds leave much to be desired, here
there are numerous toilets. They are very clean; and you are in no danger
of being urinated upon or standing in pools of urine as at some places I
could mention.
The crowd themselves are on the whole very well behaved. I have attended
about 15 events and have seen only one very minorincident. I wonder of
there is any link between the facilities offered and the behavior of the
spectators, not that this is the issue at Sheffield, although in attempting an
overview of British soccer grounds crowd behaviour sadly has to be a
considered factor. Further the number of families, and women, at sporting
events is impressively high.
The point I have reached, and I had arrived at this long before Saturday's
tragedy, is that vast improvements at soccer grounds are urgently required.
Reluctantly I have come to the conclusion that we must strive for all seater
stadia. The idea has not appealed to me in the past, indeed I was very
against Coventry City's experiment a few years ago, although part of my
  opposition was that they used it as anexcuse to raise the price of admission.
  I beleive that we can offer allseater stadia without increase in entrance fees
  above those of inflation. Please allow me tolay before you a scheme which I
  think could be put into operation.I think it should be our aim to make each
  of the 92 Football League grounds all seaters. Added to this we should
  strive to improve all aspects of spectator facilities. This, I believe will also
  add to increased safety and security. Such a scheme would involve a massive
  amount of work and also a massive sum of money. It might also require new
  grounds to be built if present ones cannot adequately be adapted.
  The cost of such a scheme might seems prohibitive, yet I think there is a
  way that it could be funded, without undue stress on national resources.
  The way I propose is that for every pound spent on a player another pound
  is paid into a central ground fund. Thus is team x buys a player for
<£100,000 they would pay the groundd fund-^100,000. This would apply on all
  transfer fees paid. A commission could be appointed to monitor transfers to
  make sure clubs were not abusing the system i.e. by using the free transfer
  system. Maybe a free transfer would involve a nominal fee to the central
  ground fund as well. This central ground fund would then be used to
  convert the grounds to all seaters. Such a system, if you like a transfer
  tax, would remain in operation until ALL grounds had been upgraded. It
  would make sense to undertake the revision of the grounds according to the
  avergae gate at this seasons league games. So the team with the highest
  average gate would be first, and so on down to the 92nd. Acceptions would
  be made for especially hazadous stadiums. I see certain advantages in such a
  scheme. First the big clubs, who pay the huge transfer fees would be the
  biggest contributors. Second, it would also help to bring some sanity to the
  transfer market.
 The C en tral G rounds fu n d could be supplim ented b y a g r e a t e r con trib u tio n
 from th e pools companies, and maybe c e rta in tax reliefs from th e governm ent
 would also come into o p eratio n.
 F u r t h e r to this scheme I would like to see the League and the FA se t an
 example b y jointly financing a new national stadiu m , fo r all s p o r ts . The NEC
 at Birmingham seems an a p p r o p r ia te place.
 I r e s p e c tf u lly ask th a t fo r the sake of o u r national game th a t su ch a scheme
 be u r g e n t ly in s tig a te d . Please.


 Yours Sincerely

                                      Dorset,        __
                                       16th April 1989


The Secretary,
Football Association,
Lancaster Gate,
London W.l



Sir,
       It is vital that the PA Cup Pinal is not cancelled.
Cancellation is a negative act and would, I am certain, "be
against the wishes of those supporters who perished so
tragically at Hi 118130rough. If there is a convincing case
for cancellation, then on the same grounds, why not call
off the remainder of the Football League programme?

The greatest tribute that can now be paid to those dead
fans is to give the Liverpool club the opportunity to go
to Wembley and win the Cup.
And whether or not Liverpool are successful in joining
Everton at Wembley, two minutes silence should be held
before the match during which the names of the dead be
read out aloud to the crowd and the nation.



Yourp. faithfully,




p.s. I would like to congratulate Mr Graham Kelly for
     the courageous dignity he displayed during the many
     post-tragedy interviews on television

               FOR THE ATTENTION OF THE CHIEF EXECUTIVE/CHAIRMAN



     While you have your debate as tp whether or not the F.A.
     should have a cup final, I (as an ordinary working housewife)
     would like to remind you that should you decide to go ahead
     by doing so you will only compound the tragic grief and


     I, personally, cannot imagine how any of you people can even
     contemplate such a decision.
       e
     Is^for the good of the game?  Is it for the loyal fans?
     or is it more likely to be for yourselves?  In any event if
     you cant ask yourselves some very searching questions and
     come up with the right answers on this occasion then God help
     the people whose money and loyalty keep you all in pontificating

     I FIND IT SICKENING.




     MRS

Dear Mr Kelly,


                   The Hillsborough Disaster


I enclose a set of proposals which could help resolve the
dreadful dilemma of whether or not to proceed with the 1989
F.A. Cup.   In essence I suggest that the Final should not be
played but that the Cup be awarded to the City of Liverpool.


I have sent a copy of these proposals to Mr Peter Robinson
of Liverpool F.C.


Yours sincerely,




end:
               A RESPONSE TO THE HILLSBOROUGH DISASTER


                             by




     The tragedy of Hillsborough has stained the F. A Cup.
Nothing can undo the event        of Saturday,    15 April 1989. The
only question is how to respond to the disaster in a manner
fitting to the memcnry of its victims and to the high ethos
of the English football game. What should          be done?
To proceed with the 1989 F.A. Cup would be insensitive to
the feelings of many,       I believe. On the other hand simply
to abandon the Cup and what it stands for          in the face   of
grief may only compound the tragedy.


     I suggest a more elaborate response.        Its aim is to erect
from the ashes of tragedy a tribute to Liverpool and English
f o o tball.



PROPOSALS


1.     Nottingham Forest shall agree to concede the semi-final
       game to Liverpool City. Thus the 1989 F.A. Cup Final teams
       shall be Liverpool and Everton.


2.     In the light of the disaster, and the coincidence that
       both Cup finalists are fjrom     Liverpool,    the F.A.   should
       a n n ounce:
               (i)    the Final shall not be played;
               (ii)   instead the Cup shall be awarded to
                      the City of Liverpool in perpetuity
                      as a token of sympathy for the loss
            and grief experienced b y the city as
            a whole, and as a tribute to the
            contribution the city has made to
            national football.


In August the Charity Shield Match shall be between
Liverpool and Everton. Proceeds from the game shall be
distributed by a specially appointed body along the
following lines:
     (i)    The commissioning of a new F.A. Cup;

     (ii)   The establishment of a fund to assist
            survivors and relatives of the dead,
            in circumstances not covered under
            provisions made by judicial awards;

     (iii) The establishment of a permanent
            "Supporters' Rights Commission", an
            independent body charged with identifying
            potential hazards for football supporters,
            and initiating proposals designed to
            advance the interests of supporters
             (rather than, for example,   the interests
            of club management, players or sponsors);

     (iv)   The establishment of a foundation to further
            the development of youth sports facilities
            in Liverpool.



   These proposals offer a just and honourable response
to a dreadful tragedy:   they offer a break from a past
which ended in horror, yet they do not constitute a
flight from that horror. The proposals are a movement
from under the shadow of the Hillsborough disaster
toward a brighter future for Liverpool and for football.




                                                   19 April 1989.

Dear Sir                       A

                HILLSBOROUGH DISASTER

                Tou must proceed with the competition. The
matches will serve as a living memorial to the grieved, dead.
                Cancellation would be psychologically sadistic
towards traumatised survivors, and wretched families and friends.
The onward process therefore must take place, albeit in sadness
and tears, and the Cup's destination duly resolved. Par crucial
reasons relating solely to minds and morales..
                What an irony when the underprivilged of
Liverpool are already such victims of government malice. Sarlier,
when South Yorkshire mineworkers united to fight for their
livlihoods, the police forces from the furthest counties appeared,
incredibly in theif thousands to brutalise them.
                Thank you for reading this.
                                            OUR REF PD/MDD



                                            W U K W -L L .H T



17th APRIL 1989                             TEL:                Evenings


Dear Mr Kelly,

I have enclosed diagrams of safety fence in view of Saturdays
horrific happening 15/4/89 at Hillsborough.

I realise these cost a lot of money, "fences" having seen the
fence at Hillsbrough and similiar around the country.  Why
cannot the existing fences be left but every other section
of fence be converted into double opening hinged gates with
a panic bar on the pitch side, which could be knocked open
by Police or a Steward at a seconds notice in case of trouble
or crushing.

Fence sections are normally 8' to 10' wide this would allow plenty
of room for specators to spill through to safety.   I should
imagine this problem mainly occurs behind the goals, so grounds
could make use of what they have got and with this alteration
of double gates, would not cost them so much, as well as
making things much safer.

Not every club is in the same position so they would have to make
alterations to suit their own needs.  I realise fences are
needed to control grounds, this way you can keep high fences
with a certain degree of safety.


Yours sincerely

Dear Mr Kelly.


M a n y p e o p l e a re u n i t e d in t h e i r d i s t r e s s a nd h o r r o r o v e r the
d e a t h and i n j u r y of so m a n y L i v e r p o o l fan s at H i l l s b o r o u g h
and w i s h to r e s p e c t t h e i r m e m o r y in som e way.



P e r h a p s a f i t t i n g m e m o r i a l to the l o v e of f o o t b a l l tha t each
of the v i c t i m s s h a r e d c o u l d be the a d d i t i o n of an
a p p r o p r i a t e e n g r a v i n g to the bas e of the F.A. C up its el f.



Thus, the m e m o r y of t h o s e w h o d i e d and w e r e i n j u r e d w h i l s t
s u p p o r t i n g t he ir b e l o v e d tearr,, w o u l d b e c o m e i n e x t r i c a b l y
l i n k e d w i t h the ver y t r o p h y the y so w i s h e d L i v e r p o o l
F o o t b a l l C l u b - t h e i r t e a m - to w i n .




Y ou rs s i n c e r e l y ,




 I am fo rwa rdi ng a copy of this letter to Mr John Smith,
 Chairman of Liverpool Football Club.
                                             Surrey.



                                             16th April 1989




Dear Sirs,

     In view of the tragic and devastating events at
Hillsborough I feel it is totally inappropriate to continue the
F.A. Cup.

     The disaster of Saturday, 15th April, has totally
overriden the football and time is most definitely required to
heal such wounds.  To continue the cup now in the wake of this
country's worst sporting accident is meaningless to us all,
despite the great traditions of the "Cup".

     Now is not the time for such emotions of football and
bereavement to be mixed; they must and can only stand alone in
respect of each other.

     In lieu of the Cup I propose that an England XI play an
F.A. XI comprising members of past F.A. Cup winning teams with
all revenue to the Trust Fund.

     Cup Football cannot be played on the fresh grave of 94
supporters.


                         Yours faithfully,

           To the kind attention of the Football Association.


                     SUGGEST 101! FOR CRO'.TD SAFETY.

I must humbly admit I have never set foot on a football ground
and moreover knov; suggestions from outsiders are usually not
heeded, but do PLEA33 consider, if it is at all feasable, this
  G 3. t
'THAT ABOUT BRE.-JK-CRO'.DS, i.e. strong barriers embedded in
concrete and staggered?




cro'.vd pushing




The danger of cro :ds is the strength of the numbers pushing.


                                         a British subject living
                                                   in Belgiun.
Sunday April lEth, 19B9.                            U.S.A.




YOU BASTARDS - You money hungry bastards...
                                           My paper today shows
pictures of young innocent football supporters being crushed to
death against your monstrous railings in the debacle showpiece at
Hillsbourgh, Sheffield.


                                 My god - what a spectacle... After the long
line ofo r c h e s t r a t e d atrocities in soccer during the past lO^years,
conducted in the name of sport - you elect to ignore the basic
principles of crowd control and safety - for the sake of money.


                       The most simple solution to major game
attractions today is an all ticket game - andtelevise the event
live - thereby negating the crowd factor. But you elect to
"black-out” the populace, and go for the live gate booty.


                       I hope and pray the government takes
action to halt the insatiable appetite of the Football Association.
Meanwhile, the many young lives lost in Sheffield are beyond
recovery.


                       My letter to Margaret Thatcher will be
more demanding... You morons are blind to the facts ~ and immersed
in the world of profits and lass (of lives).


                           tp Qn you faceless, nameless cowards.
                           May your souls roast in hell.

                                                                       Lancs.,


                                                                       23rd April 1989

Dear Sir,
            1 a m writing this letter as a life-long football supporter
who has stood on the Stretford End at Manchester United every other
Saturday for the last 16 years.   I have attended 4 FA Cup Finals and 5
Semi-finals Including 2 matches at Hillsborough.   I trust that you will
take note of the following observations before making any hasty
decisions about the future of our national game.

The p r o b l e m o utside the gro u n d at H i l l s b o r o u g h.

1 believe that the 2 main factors here were:-

     a)    The late arrival of a large number of supporters.
     b)    Fans without tickets attempting to gain entrance to the ground.

Late arrival Is caused by people staying In the pub until what they
Judge to be the last possible moment.  In this Instance traffic
congestion may have exacerbated the situation.  It could beargued   that
the ban on the sale of alcohol Inside football grounds leads to people
arriving late.

Fans arriving without tickets are a major problem.  I personally
remember having to struggle to reach the turnstiles past milling fans
who have no tickets.  The latter are largely Intent on climbing over
walls or fences, breaking through weak gates or crawling under the
turnstiles.  The only solution appears to be establishing an outer
barrier or cordon where tickets must be shown to gain access to the
turnstiles.

At this point I must say that ID cards would only have added to the
problems at Hillsborough.  If ID cards are Introduced I suggest that for
all-ticket games the ID cards should be shown when purchasing the
tickets with no further need for them to be shown when entering the
ground.  An added advantage of this suggestion would be that ticket
touts would be unable to easily acquire more than one ticket.

The problem lafilde the ground a t Hillsborough
Whatever the conclusions of the official Inquiries I aa in no doubt that
the decision to open the large external gate was the ultimate cause of
the tragedy.   The surge of people Into the centre section of terrace
behind the goal was probably sufficient to finally overload an already
over-crowded section.   At Hillsborough the problem m s not helped by the
fact that the entrance to the terracing behind the goal m s centrally
situated.   If the entrancss were near the corner flags the crowd would
have to make Its way laterally to get directly behind the goal.   Vlth
effective stewarding this is a perfectly safe arrangement in that as
each section becomes full it can be closed and thus late arrivals have
to stand nearer the corner flags.    1 aai obviously unable to comment on
the level of stewarding at Hillsborough but the    very uneven distribution
of spectators at the Leppings Lane End suggests that it was inadequate.

Conclusions

1.   Fans without tickets must be denied access to the vicinity of the
     ground at all-ticket games.

2.   With the segregation and fencing that exists at most major grounds
     effective stewarding will prevent any over-crowding.

3.   I believe that fences around the pitch should remain to prevent the
     odd idiot running onto the pitch.

4.   I cannot see how ID cards would have prevented this tragedy or
     would stop people travelling to matches without tickets.

5.     To finish on a personal note 1 would not go to an all-seater ground
     as I believe it would lack any atmosphere.


                     yours sincerely,




Copies to :-

     Hr. D. Hurd CMP)
     Hr. R. Hattersley CMP)
     Mr. M. Jack CMP)
     Mr. G. Kelly FA
     Chief Constable, S.Yorkshire
